<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Rabies</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<p><b>Rabies</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Viral_disease" title="Viral disease">viral disease</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">inflammation of the brain</a> in humans and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Early symptoms can include fever and tingling at the site of exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> These symptoms are followed by one or more of the following symptoms: violent movements, uncontrolled excitement, fear of water, an inability to move parts of the body, confusion, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_consciousness" title="Loss of consciousness">loss of consciousness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Once symptoms appear, the result is nearly always death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> The time period between contracting the disease and the start of symptoms is usually one to three months; however, this time period can vary from less than one week to more than one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> The time is dependent on the distance the virus must travel to reach the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rabies is caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Lyssavirus" title="Lyssavirus">lyssaviruses</a></i>, including <a href="/wiki/Rabies_virus" title="Rabies virus">rabies virus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_bat_lyssavirus" title="Australian bat lyssavirus">Australian bat lyssavirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Department_of_Health_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Department_of_Health-3">[3]</a></sup> Rabies is spread when an infected animal scratches or bites another animal or human.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Saliva from an infected animal can also transmit rabies if the saliva comes into contact with the eyes, mouth, or nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Globally, dogs are the most common animal involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> More than 99% of rabies cases in countries where <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a> commonly have the disease are caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dog_bites" title="Dog bites">dog bites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint2010_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tint2010-4">[4]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bat</a> bites are the most common source of rabies infections in humans, and less than 5% of cases are from dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint2010_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tint2010-4">[4]</a></sup> Rodents are very rarely infected with rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tint2010_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tint2010-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rabies_virus" title="Rabies virus">rabies virus</a> travels to the brain by following the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nerves</a>. The disease can only be diagnosed after the start of symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Animal control and vaccination programs have decreased the risk of rabies from dogs in a number of regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Immunizing people before they are exposed is recommended for those who are at high risk. The high-risk group includes people who work with bats or who spend prolonged periods in areas of the world where rabies is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> In people who have been exposed to rabies, the <a href="/wiki/Rabies_vaccine" title="Rabies vaccine">rabies vaccine</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Rabies_immunoglobulin" title="Rabies immunoglobulin">rabies immunoglobulin</a> are effective in preventing the disease if the person receives the treatment before the start of rabies symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Washing bites and scratches for 15 minutes with soap and water, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Povidone_iodine" title="Povidone iodine">povidone iodine</a>, or detergent may reduce the number of viral particles and may be somewhat effective at preventing transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Only five people have survived a rabies infection after showing symptoms, and this was with extensive treatment known as the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_protocol" title="Milwaukee protocol">Milwaukee protocol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rabies causes about 24,000 to 60,000 deaths worldwide per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> More than 95% of human deaths caused by rabies occur in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> Rabies is present in more than 150 countries and on all continents but Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> More than 3 billion people live in regions of the world where rabies occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup> A number of countries, including Australia, Canada, Japan, and the United States, and Western Europe, do not have rabies among dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Many small island nations do not have rabies at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabies_patient.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2243" data-file-width="3300" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rabies_patient.jpg/220px-Rabies_patient.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rabies_patient.jpg/330px-Rabies_patient.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Rabies_patient.jpg/440px-Rabies_patient.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabies_patient.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A person with rabies, 1959</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">period</a> between infection and the first <a href="/wiki/Influenza-like_illness" title="Influenza-like illness">symptoms</a> (incubation period) is typically 1–3 months in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Incubation_period" title="Incubation period">Incubation periods</a> as short as four days and longer than six years have been documented, depending on the location and severity of the contaminated wound and the amount of virus introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> Initial signs and symptoms of rabies are often nonspecific such as fever and headache.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> As rabies progresses and causes <a href="/wiki/Meningoencephalitis" title="Meningoencephalitis">inflammation of the brain and/or meninges</a>, signs and symptoms can include slight or partial <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">anxiety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confusion" title="Confusion">confusion</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/restlessness" title="wikt:restlessness">agitation</a>, abnormal behavior, <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a>, terror, and <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a>, progressing to <a href="/wiki/Delirium" title="Delirium">delirium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> The person may also have hydrophobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Death usually occurs 2 to 10 days after first symptoms. Survival is rare once symptoms have presented,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> even with the administration of proper and intensive care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Expert_Review_of_Anti-infective_Therapy_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Expert_Review_of_Anti-infective_Therapy-13">[13]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeanna_Giese" title="Jeanna Giese">Jeanna Giese</a>, who in 2004 was the first patient treated with the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_protocol" title="Milwaukee protocol">Milwaukee protocol</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lite2009_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lite2009-14">[14]</a></sup> became the first person ever recorded to have survived rabies without receiving successful <a href="/wiki/Post-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Post-exposure prophylaxis">post-exposure prophylaxis</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Intention-to-treat_analysis" title="Intention-to-treat analysis">intention-to-treat analysis</a> has since found this protocol has a survival rate of about 8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2217.2Ffvl.09.52_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2217.2Ffvl.09.52-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrophobia">Hydrophobia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hydrophobia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabid_dog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2118" data-file-width="2728" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Rabid_dog.jpg/220px-Rabid_dog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Rabid_dog.jpg/330px-Rabid_dog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Rabid_dog.jpg/440px-Rabid_dog.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabid_dog.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rabid dog</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrophobia ("fear of water") is the historic name for rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It refers to a set of symptoms in the later stages of an infection in which the person has difficulty swallowing, shows panic when presented with liquids to drink, and cannot quench his or her thirst. Any mammal infected with the virus may demonstrate hydrophobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHS-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saliva production is greatly increased, and attempts to drink, or even the intention or suggestion of drinking, may cause excruciatingly painful spasms of the muscles in the throat and <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>. This can be attributed to the fact that the virus multiplies and assimilates in the <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a> of the infected animal for the purpose of further transmission through biting. The ability to transmit the virus would decrease significantly if the infected individual could swallow saliva and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hydrophobia is commonly associated with furious rabies, which affects 80% of rabies-infected people. The remaining 20% may experience a paralytic form of rabies that is marked by muscle weakness, loss of sensation, and paralysis; this form of rabies does not usually cause fear of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHS_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHS-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cause">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="1835" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG/220px-Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG/330px-Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG/440px-Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabies_Virus_EM_PHIL_1876.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">TEM</a> <a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">micrograph</a> with numerous rabies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virion" title="Virion">virions</a> (small, dark grey, rodlike particles) and <a href="/wiki/Negri_bodies" title="Negri bodies">Negri bodies</a> (the larger <a href="/wiki/Pathognomonic" title="Pathognomonic">pathognomonic</a> cellular inclusions of rabies infection)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rabies is caused by a number of <i><a href="/wiki/Lyssavirus" title="Lyssavirus">lyssaviruses</a></i> including: <a href="/wiki/Rabies_virus" title="Rabies virus">rabies virus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_bat_lyssavirus" title="Australian bat lyssavirus">Australian bat lyssavirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Department_of_Health_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Department_of_Health-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rabies virus is the <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Lyssavirus" title="Lyssavirus">Lyssavirus</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a>, in the family <i><a href="/wiki/Rhabdoviridae" title="Rhabdoviridae">Rhabdoviridae</a></i>, order <i><a href="/wiki/Mononegavirales" title="Mononegavirales">Mononegavirales</a></i>. Lyssavirions have helical symmetry, with a length of about 180 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> and a cross-section of about 75 nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-19">[19]</a></sup> These virions are <a href="/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">enveloped</a> and have a single-stranded <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> genome with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative-sense#Negative-sense" title="Negative-sense">negative sense</a>. The genetic information is packed as a <a href="/wiki/Ribonucleoprotein" title="Ribonucleoprotein">ribonucleoprotein</a> complex in which RNA is tightly bound by the viral nucleoprotein. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_genome" title="RNA genome">RNA genome</a> of the virus encodes five genes whose order is highly conserved: nucleoprotein (N), phosphoprotein (P), matrix protein (M), glycoprotein (G), and the viral RNA polymerase (L).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15885837_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15885837-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once within a muscle or nerve cell, the virus undergoes replication. The trimeric spikes on the exterior of the membrane of the virus interact with a specific cell receptor, the most likely one being the <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">acetylcholine</a> receptor. The cellular membrane pinches in a procession known as <a href="/wiki/Pinocytosis" title="Pinocytosis">pinocytosis</a> and allows entry of the virus into the cell by way of an <a href="/wiki/Endosome" title="Endosome">endosome</a>. The virus then uses the acidic environment, which is necessary, of that endosome and binds to its membrane simultaneously, releasing its five proteins and single strand RNA into the cytoplasm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The L protein then transcribes five mRNA strands and a positive strand of RNA all from the original negative strand RNA using free nucleotides in the cytoplasm. These five mRNA strands are then translated into their corresponding proteins (P, L, N, G and M proteins) at free ribosomes in the cytoplasm. Some proteins require post-translative modifications. For example, the G protein travels through the rough <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>, where it undergoes further folding, and is then transported to the <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a>, where a sugar group is added to it (<a href="/wiki/Glycosylation" title="Glycosylation">glycosylation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Where there are enough proteins, the viral polymerase will begin to synthesize new negative strands of RNA from the template of the positive strand RNA. These negative strands will then form complexes with the N, P, L and M proteins and then travel to the inner membrane of the cell, where a G protein has embedded itself in the membrane. The G protein then coils around the N-P-L-M complex of proteins taking some of the host cell membrane with it, which will form the new outer envelope of the virus particle. The virus then buds from the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the point of entry, the virus is <a href="/wiki/Neurotropic_virus" title="Neurotropic virus">neurotropic</a>, traveling quickly along the neural pathways into the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>. The virus usually first infects muscle cells close to the site of infection, where they are able to replicate without being 'noticed' by the host's immune system. Once enough virus has been replicated, they begin to bind to <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine_receptor" title="Acetylcholine receptor">acetylcholine receptors</a> (p75NR) at the neuromuscular junction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The virus then travels through the nerve cell axon via retrograde transport, as its P protein interacts with dynein, a protein present in the cytoplasm of nerve cells. Once the virus reaches the cell body it travels rapidly to the Central Nervous System (CNS), replicating in motor neurons and eventually reaching the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robbins_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robbins-2">[2]</a></sup> After the brain is infected, the virus travels centrifugally to the peripheral and autonomic nervous systems, eventually migrating to the salivary glands, where it is ready to be transmitted to the next host.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baer1991_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baer1991-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:317</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Transmission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rabies_transmission" title="Rabies transmission">Rabies transmission</a></div>
<p>All warm-blooded species, including humans, may become infected with the rabies virus and develop symptoms. <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">Birds</a> were first artificially infected with rabies in 1884; however, infected birds are largely, if not wholly, asymptomatic, and recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serological_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-serological-24">[24]</a></sup> Other bird species have been known to develop rabies <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>, a sign of infection, after feeding on rabies-infected mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16498885_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16498885-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owls_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owls-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The virus has also adapted to grow in cells of <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">poikilothermic</a> ("cold-blooded") vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Most animals can be infected by the virus and can transmit the disease to humans. Infected <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid13858519_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid13858519-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14431118_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14431118-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Monkey" title="Monkey">monkeys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skunk" title="Skunk">skunks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coyotes" title="Coyotes">coyotes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongooses</a> (normally yellow mongoose)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a> present the greatest risk to humans.</p>
<p>Rabies may also spread through exposure to infected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bears" title="Bears">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">domestic farm animals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groundhog" title="Groundhog">groundhogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">wild carnivorans</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lagomorphs" title="Lagomorphs">Lagomorphs</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>, and small <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Chipmunk" title="Chipmunk">chipmunks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerbil" title="Gerbil">gerbils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mice" title="Mice">mice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a>, are almost never found to be infected with rabies and are not known to transmit rabies to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Bites from mice, rats, or squirrels rarely require rabies prevention because these rodents are typically killed by any encounter with a larger, rabid animal, and would, therefore, not be carriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Virginia_opossum" title="Virginia opossum">Virginia opossum</a> is resistant but not immune to rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The virus is usually present in the nerves and <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a> of a symptomatic rabid animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The route of <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> is usually, but not always, by a bite. In many cases, the infected animal is exceptionally aggressive, may attack without provocation, and exhibits otherwise uncharacteristic behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This is an example of a viral pathogen <a href="/wiki/Behavior-altering_parasites_and_parasitoids" title="Behavior-altering parasites and parasitoids">modifying the behavior of its host</a> to facilitate its transmission to other hosts.</p>
<p>Transmission between humans is extremely rare. A few cases have been recorded through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplant" title="Organ transplant">transplant surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The only well-documented cases of rabies caused by human-to-human transmission occurred among eight recipients of transplanted corneas and among three recipients of solid organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In addition to transmission from cornea and organ transplants, bite and non-bite exposures inflicted by infected humans could theoretically transmit rabies, but no such cases have been documented, since infected humans are usually hospitalized and necessary precautions taken. Casual contact, such as touching a person with rabies or contact with non-infectious fluid or tissue (urine, blood, feces) does not constitute an exposure and does not require post-exposure prophylaxis. Additionally, as the virus is present in sperm or vaginal secretions, spread through sex may be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a typical human infection by bite, the virus enters the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nervous system</a>. It then travels along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afferent_nerve_fiber" title="Afferent nerve fiber">afferent nerves</a> toward the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> During this phase, the virus cannot be easily detected within the host, and vaccination may still confer cell-mediated immunity to prevent symptomatic rabies. When the virus reaches the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, it rapidly causes <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a>, the prodromal phase, which is the beginning of the symptoms. Once the patient becomes symptomatic, treatment is almost never effective and mortality is over 99%. Rabies may also inflame the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Transverse_myelitis" title="Transverse myelitis">transverse myelitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LynnNewton2012_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-LynnNewton2012-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rabies can be difficult to diagnose, because, in the early stages, it is easily confused with other diseases or with aggressiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-44">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard_(test)" title="Gold standard (test)">reference method</a> for diagnosing rabies is the fluorescent antibody test (FAT), an <a href="/wiki/Immunohistochemistry" title="Immunohistochemistry">immunohistochemistry</a> procedure, which is recommended by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The FAT relies on the ability of a detector molecule (usually fluorescein isothiocyanate) coupled with a rabies-specific antibody, forming a conjugate, to bind to and allow the visualisation of rabies antigen using fluorescent microscopy techniques. Microscopic analysis of samples is the only direct method that allows for the identification of rabies virus-specific antigen in a short time and at a reduced cost, irrespective of geographical origin and status of the host. It has to be regarded as the first step in diagnostic procedures for all laboratories. Autolysed samples can, however, reduce the sensitivity and specificity of the FAT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fooks_AR_2009_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fooks_AR_2009-46">[46]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription_polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Reverse transcription polymerase chain reaction">RT PCR</a> assays proved to be a sensitive and specific tool for routine diagnostic purposes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> particularly in decomposed samples<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12034539_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12034539-48">[48]</a></sup> or archival specimens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17881871_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17881871-49">[49]</a></sup> The diagnosis can be reliably made from brain samples taken after death. The diagnosis can also be made from saliva, urine, and cerebrospinal fluid samples, but this is not as <a href="/wiki/Sensitivity_and_specificity" title="Sensitivity and specificity">sensitive</a> and reliable as brain samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fooks_AR_2009_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fooks_AR_2009-46">[46]</a></sup> Cerebral inclusion bodies called <a href="/wiki/Negri_bodies" title="Negri bodies">Negri bodies</a> are 100% diagnostic for rabies infection but are found in only about 80% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-19">[19]</a></sup> If possible, the animal from which the bite was received should also be examined for rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ly2009_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ly2009-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_microscopy#Optical_microscopy" title="Light microscopy">light microscopy</a> techniques may also be used to diagnose rabies at a tenth of the cost of traditional fluorescence microscopy techniques, allowing identification of the disease in less-developed countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Differential diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">differential diagnosis</a> in a case of suspected human rabies may initially include any cause of <a href="/wiki/Encephalitis" title="Encephalitis">encephalitis</a>, in particular infection with viruses such as <a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">herpesviruses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enteroviruses" title="Enteroviruses">enteroviruses</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arboviruses" title="Arboviruses">arboviruses</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>. The most important viruses to rule out are <a href="/wiki/Herpes_simplex_virus" title="Herpes simplex virus">herpes simplex virus</a> type one, <a href="/wiki/Varicella_zoster_virus" title="Varicella zoster virus">varicella zoster virus</a>, and (less commonly) enteroviruses, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coxsackie_virus" title="Coxsackie virus">coxsackieviruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echovirus" title="Echovirus">echoviruses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">polioviruses</a>, and human <a href="/wiki/Enterovirus" title="Enterovirus">enteroviruses</a> 68 to 71.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>New causes of viral encephalitis are also possible, as was evidenced by the 1999 outbreak in Malaysia of 300 cases of encephalitis with a mortality rate of 40% caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nipah_virus" title="Nipah virus">Nipah virus</a>, a newly recognized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramyxovirus" title="Paramyxovirus">paramyxovirus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-refDiseasesOfSwine_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-refDiseasesOfSwine-53">[53]</a></sup> Likewise, well-known viruses may be introduced into new locales, as is illustrated by the outbreak of encephalitis due to West Nile virus in the eastern United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Epidemiologic factors, such as season, geographic location, and the patient's age, travel history, and possible exposure to bites, rodents, and ticks, may help direct the diagnosis.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Almost all human cases of rabies were fatal until a vaccine was developed in 1885 by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Roux" title="Émile Roux">Émile Roux</a>. Their original vaccine was harvested from infected rabbits, from which the virus in the nerve tissue was weakened by allowing it to dry for five to ten days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Similar nerve tissue-derived vaccines are still used in some countries, as they are much cheaper than modern cell culture vaccines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15069272_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15069272-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The human diploid cell rabies vaccine was started in 1967. Less expensive purified chicken embryo cell vaccine and purified <a href="/wiki/Vero_cell" title="Vero cell">vero cell</a> rabies vaccine are now available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ly2009_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ly2009-50">[50]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recombinant_vaccine" title="Recombinant vaccine">recombinant vaccine</a> called V-RG has been used in Belgium, France, Germany, and the United States to prevent outbreaks of rabies in undomesticated animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reece_2006_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reece_2006-57">[57]</a></sup> Immunization before exposure has been used in both human and nonhuman populations, where, as in many jurisdictions, domesticated animals are required to be vaccinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of recorded human deaths from rabies in the United States has dropped from 100 or more annually in the early 20th century to one or two per year due to widespread vaccination of domestic dogs and cats and the development of human vaccines and immunoglobulin treatments. Most deaths now result from bat bites, which may go unnoticed by the victim and hence untreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_Epi_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_Epi-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Missouri Department of Health and Senior Services Communicable Disease Surveillance 2007 Annual Report states the following can help reduce the risk of contracting rabies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Vaccinating dogs, cats, and ferrets against rabies</li>
<li>Keeping pets under supervision</li>
<li>Not handling wild animals or strays</li>
<li>Contacting an animal control officer upon observing a wild animal or a stray, especially if the animal is acting strangely</li>
<li>If bitten by an animal, washing the wound with soap and water for 10 to 15 minutes and contacting a healthcare provider to determine if post-exposure prophylaxis is required</li>
</ul>
<p>September 28 is <a href="/wiki/World_Rabies_Day" title="World Rabies Day">World Rabies Day</a>, which promotes the information, prevention, and elimination of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postexposure_prophylaxis" title="Postexposure prophylaxis">Treatment after exposure</a> can prevent the disease if administered promptly, generally within 10 days of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherris_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sherris-19">[19]</a></sup> Thoroughly washing the wound as soon as possible with soap and water for approximately five minutes is effective in reducing the number of viral particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Povidone-iodine" title="Povidone-iodine">Povidone-iodine</a> or alcohol is then recommended to reduce the virus further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> recommends people receive one dose of human rabies <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin" title="Immunoglobulin">immunoglobulin</a> (HRIG) and four doses of rabies vaccine over a 14-day period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The immunoglobulin dose should not exceed 20 units per kilogram body weight. HRIG is expensive and constitutes most of the cost of post exposure treatment, ranging as high as several thousand dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> As much as possible of this dose should be injected around the bites, with the remainder being given by deep intramuscular injection at a site distant from the vaccination site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first dose of rabies vaccine is given as soon as possible after exposure, with additional doses on days three, seven and 14 after the first. Patients who have previously received pre-exposure vaccination do not receive the immunoglobulin, only the postexposure vaccinations on days 0 and 3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pain and side effects of modern cell-based vaccines are similar to flu shots. The old nerve-tissue-based vaccinations that require multiple painful injections into the abdomen with a large needle are inexpensive, but are being phased out and replaced by affordable World Health Organization intradermal-vaccination regimens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ly2009_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ly2009-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Intramuscular vaccination should be given into the <a href="/wiki/Deltoid_muscle" title="Deltoid muscle">deltoid</a>, not the <a href="/wiki/Gluteal_muscles" title="Gluteal muscles">gluteal area</a>, which has been associated with vaccination failure due to injection into fat rather than muscle. In infants, the lateral thigh is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Awakening to find a bat in the room, or finding a bat in the room of a previously unattended child or mentally disabled or intoxicated person, is regarded as an indication for <a href="/wiki/Post-exposure_prophylaxis" title="Post-exposure prophylaxis">post-exposure prophylaxis</a> (PEP). The recommendation for the precautionary use of PEP in occult bat encounters where no contact is recognized has been questioned in the medical literature, based on a <a href="/wiki/Cost%E2%80%93benefit_analysis" title="Cost–benefit analysis">cost–benefit analysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, a 2002 study has supported the protocol of precautionary administering of PEP where a child or mentally compromised individual has been alone with a bat, especially in sleep areas, where a bite or exposure may occur without the victim being aware.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Begun with little or no delay, PEP is 100% effective against rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lite2009_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lite2009-14">[14]</a></sup> In the case in which there has been a significant delay in administering PEP, the treatment should be administered regardless, as it may still be effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Induced_coma">Induced coma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Induced coma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_protocol" title="Milwaukee protocol">Milwaukee protocol</a></div>
<p>In 2004, American teenager Jeanna Giese survived an infection of rabies unvaccinated. She was placed into an <a href="/wiki/Induced_coma" title="Induced coma">induced coma</a> upon onset of symptoms and given <a href="/wiki/Ketamine" title="Ketamine">ketamine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midazolam" title="Midazolam">midazolam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ribavirin" title="Ribavirin">ribavirin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a>. Her doctors administered treatment based on the hypothesis that detrimental effects of rabies were caused by temporary dysfunctions in the brain and could be avoided by inducing a temporary partial halt in brain function that would protect the brain from damage while giving the immune system time to defeat the virus. After 31 days of isolation and 76 days of hospitalization, Giese was released from the hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15958806_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15958806-70">[70]</a></sup> She survived with all higher level brain functions, but an inability to walk and balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> On a podcast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" title="National Public Radio">NPR</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Radiolab" title="Radiolab">Radiolab</a></i>, Giese recounted: "I had to learn how to stand and then to walk, turn around, move my toes. I was really, after rabies, a new born baby who couldn't do anything. I had to relearn that all...mentally I knew how to do stuff but my body wouldn't cooperate with what I wanted it to do. It definitely took a toll on me psychologically. You know I'm still recovering. I'm not completely back. Stuff like balance, and I can't run normally."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Giese's treatment regimen became known as the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_protocol" title="Milwaukee protocol">Milwaukee protocol</a>, which has since undergone revision with the second version omitting the use of ribavirin. Two of 25 patients survived when treated under the first protocol. A further 10 patients have been treated under the revised protocol, with a further two survivors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2217.2Ffvl.09.52_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2217.2Ffvl.09.52-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 12, 2011, Precious Reynolds, an eight-year-old girl from <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_County,_California" title="Humboldt County, California">Humboldt County, California</a>, became the third reported person in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> to have recovered from rabies without receiving PEP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In unvaccinated humans, rabies is almost always fatal after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurological" title="Neurological">neurological</a> symptoms have developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_factsheet_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_factsheet-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccination</a> after exposure, PEP, is highly successful in preventing the disease if administered promptly, in general within 6 days of infection. Begun with little or no delay, PEP is 100% effective against rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lite2009_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lite2009-14">[14]</a></sup> In the case of significant delay in administering PEP, the treatment still has a chance of success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Rabies_PEP_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Rabies_PEP-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Five of the first 43 patients (12%) treated with the Milwaukee protocol survived, and those receiving treatment survived longer than those not receiving the treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prevalence_of_rabies" title="Prevalence of rabies">Prevalence of rabies</a></div>

<span style="border:thin solid black"><span style="border-left:1.2em solid;border-left-color: #CAF9DB" title="#CAF9DB">﻿</span></span> year of rabies elimination unknown</div>

<p>In 2010, an estimated 26,000 people died from rabies, down from 54,000 in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loz2012_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loz2012-76">[76]</a></sup> The majority of the deaths occurred in Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_factsheet_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_factsheet-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> has the highest rate of human rabies in the world, primarily because of stray dogs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> whose number has greatly increased since a 2001 law forbade the killing of dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris-78">[78]</a></sup> Effective control and treatment of rabies in India is also hindered by a form of <a href="/wiki/Mass_hysteria" title="Mass hysteria">mass hysteria</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Puppy_pregnancy_syndrome" title="Puppy pregnancy syndrome">puppy pregnancy syndrome</a> (PPS). Dog bite victims with PPS (both male and female) become convinced that puppies are growing inside them, and often seek help from faith healers rather than from conventional medical services. In cases where the bite was from a rabid dog, this decision can prove fatal. Dr. Nitai Kishore Marik, former district medical officer of West Midnapur, states "I have seen scores of cases of rabies that reached our hospitals very late because of the intervention of faith healers. We could not save those lives."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> An estimated 20,000 people die every year from rabies in India — more than a third of the global toll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-harris-78">[78]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, China had the second-highest number of cases (approximately 6,000), followed by the Democratic Republic of the Congo (5,600).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rabies virus survives in widespread, varied, rural animal reservoirs. Despite Australia's official rabies-free status,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australian_bat_lyssavirus" title="Australian bat lyssavirus">Australian bat lyssavirus</a> (ABLV), discovered in 1996, is a strain of rabies prevalent in native bat populations. There have been three human cases of ABLV in Australia, all of them fatal.</p>
<p>In Asia and in parts of the Americas and Africa, dogs remain the principal host. Mandatory vaccination of animals is less effective in rural areas. Especially in developing countries, pets may not be privately kept and their destruction may be unacceptable. Oral vaccines can be safely distributed in baits, a practice that has successfully reduced rabies in rural areas of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montr%C3%A9al" title="Montréal">Montréal</a>, Quebec, Canada, baits are successfully used on raccoons in the Mont-Royal Park area. Vaccination campaigns may be expensive, and cost-benefit analysis suggests baits may be a cost-effective method of control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, a dramatic drop in rabies was recorded when an aerial bait-vaccination campaign was launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grambo_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grambo-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rabies is common among wild animals in the US. <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">Bats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skunk" title="Skunk">skunks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a> account for almost all reported cases (98% in 2009). Rabid bats are found in all 48 contiguous states. Other reservoirs are more limited geographically; for example, the raccoon rabies virus variant is only found in a relatively narrow band along the East Coast. Due to a high public awareness of the virus, efforts at vaccination of domestic animals and curtailment of feral populations, and availability of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postexposure_prophylaxis" title="Postexposure prophylaxis">postexposure prophylaxis</a>, incidents of rabies in humans are very rare. A total of 49 cases of the disease was reported in the country between 1995 and 2011; of these, 11 are thought to have been acquired abroad. Almost all domestically acquired cases are attributed to bat bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, the disease has been virtually eliminated after scientists placed chicken heads laced with live attenuated vaccine in the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Alps" title="Swiss Alps">Swiss Alps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grambo_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grambo-83">[83]</a></sup> The foxes of Switzerland, proven to be the main source of rabies in the country, ate the chicken heads and immunized themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grambo_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grambo-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, after being declared rabies-free from 1997 to 2008, has witnessed a reemergence of the disease in wild animals in the <a href="/wiki/Triveneto" title="Triveneto">Triveneto</a> regions (<a href="/wiki/Trentino-Alto_Adige/S%C3%BCdtirol" title="Trentino-Alto Adige/Südtirol">Trentino-Alto Adige/Südtirol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veneto" title="Veneto">Veneto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friuli-Venezia_Giulia" title="Friuli-Venezia Giulia">Friuli-Venezia Giulia</a>), due to the spreading of an epidemic in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> that hit <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> too. An extensive wild animals vaccination campaign eliminated the virus from Italy again, and it regained the rabies-free country status in 2013, the last reported case of rabies being reported in a red fox in early 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1164" data-file-width="2043" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg/250px-Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg/375px-Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg/500px-Middle_Ages_rabid_dog.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sauvages_de_la_Croix,_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage,_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1352" data-file-width="1034" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg/220px-Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg/330px-Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg/440px-Sauvages_de_la_Croix%2C_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage%2C_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sauvages_de_la_Croix,_Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_%E2%80%93_Dissertation_sur_la_nature_et_la_cause_de_la_Rage,_1777_%E2%80%93_BEIC_3001126.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Boissier_de_Sauvages_de_Lacroix" title="François Boissier de Sauvages de Lacroix">François Boissier de Sauvages de Lacroix</a>, <i>Della natura e causa della rabbia</i> (<i>Dissertation sur la nature et la cause de la Rage</i>), 1777</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rabies has been known since around 2000 B.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The first written record of rabies is in the Mesopotamian <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Eshnunna" title="Laws of Eshnunna">Codex of Eshnunna</a> (circa 1930 BC), which dictates that the owner of a dog showing symptoms of rabies should take preventive measure against bites. If another person were bitten by a rabid dog and later died, the owner was heavily fined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ineffective folk remedies abounded in the medical literature of the ancient world. The physician <a href="/wiki/Scribonius_Largus" title="Scribonius Largus">Scribonius Largus</a> prescribed a poultice of cloth and hyena skin; <a href="/wiki/Antaeus_(physician)" title="Antaeus (physician)">Antaeus</a> recommended a preparation made from the skull of a hanged man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rabies appears to have originated in the Old World, the first <a href="/wiki/Epizootic" title="Epizootic">epizootic</a> in the New World occurring in Boston in 1768.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> It spread from there, over the next few years, to various other states, as well as to the French West Indies, eventually becoming common all across North America.</p>
<p>Rabies was considered a scourge for its prevalence in the 19th century. In France and Belgium, where <a href="/wiki/Hubertus" title="Hubertus">Saint Hubert</a> was venerated, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Hubert%27s_Key" title="St Hubert's Key">St Hubert's Key</a>" was heated and applied to cauterize the wound. By an application of <a href="/wiki/Magical_thinking" title="Magical thinking">magical thinking</a>, dogs were branded with the key in hopes of protecting them from rabies. The fear of rabies was almost irrational, due to the insignificant number of vectors (mostly rabid dogs) and the absence of any efficacious treatment. It was not uncommon for a person bitten by a dog but merely suspected of being rabid, to commit suicide or to be killed by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rotivel_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rotivel-91">[91]</a></sup> This gave <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> ample opportunity to test postexposure treatments from 1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giesen2015_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Giesen2015-8">[8]</a></sup> In ancient times, the attachment of the tongue (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingual_frenulum" title="Lingual frenulum">lingual frenulum</a>, a mucous membrane) was cut and removed as this is where rabies was thought to originate. This practice ceased with the discovery of the actual cause of rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baer1991_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baer1991-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern times, the fear of rabies has not diminished, and the disease and its symptoms, particularly agitation has served as an <a href="/wiki/Rabies_in_popular_culture" title="Rabies in popular culture">inspiration for several works</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zombie" title="Zombie">zombie</a> or similarly-themed fiction, often portraying rabies as having mutated into a stronger virus which fills humans with murderous rage or uncurable illness, bringing about a devastating, widespread pandemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>rabies</i>, "madness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SimpsonDP1979_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-SimpsonDP1979-93">[93]</a></sup> This, in turn, may be related to the Sanskrit <i>rabhas</i>, "to do violence".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The Greeks derived the word <i>lyssa</i>, from <i>lud</i> or "violent"; this root is used in the genus name of the rabies virus, <i>Lyssavirus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rotivel_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rotivel-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rabies_in_animals" title="Rabies in animals">Rabies in animals</a></div>
<p>Rabies is infectious to <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>; three stages of central nervous system infection are recognized. The first stage is a one- to three-day period characterized by behavioral changes and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Prodrome" title="Prodrome">prodromal stage</a>. The second is the excitative stage, which lasts three to four days. This stage is often known as "furious rabies" for the tendency of the affected animal to be hyper-reactive to external stimuli and bite at anything near. The third is the paralytic stage and is caused by damage to <a href="/wiki/Motor_neuron" title="Motor neuron">motor neurons</a>. Incoordination is seen, owing to rear limb <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>, and drooling and difficulty swallowing is caused by paralysis of facial and throat muscles. Death is usually caused by <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_arrest" title="Respiratory arrest">respiratory arrest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ettinger_1995_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ettinger_1995-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rabies&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rabies has the advantage over other <a href="/wiki/Pseudotyping" title="Pseudotyping">pseudotyping</a> methods for gene delivery in that the cell-targeting (<a href="/wiki/Tissue_tropism" title="Tissue tropism">tissue tropism</a>) is more specific for difficult-to-reach sites, such as the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> without invasive delivery methods, as well as being capable of <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_tracing" title="Retrograde tracing">retrograde tracing</a> (i.e., going against the flow of information at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapses" title="Synapses">synapses</a>) in neuronal circuits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence indicates artificially increasing the permeability of the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, which normally does not allow most immune cells across, promotes viral clearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_2007b_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_2007b-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy_2007a_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy_2007a-98">[98]</a></sup></p>

Virol</i>. <b>81</b> (3): 1110–8. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1128%2FJVI.01964-06" rel="nofollow">10.1128/JVI.01964-06</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a> <span class="plainlinks"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1797506" rel="nofollow">1797506</a><span style="margin-left:0.1em"><img alt="Freely accessible" data-file-height="813" data-file-width="512" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/14px-Lock-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/18px-Lock-green.svg.png 2x" title="Freely accessible" width="9"/></span></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/17108029" rel="nofollow">17108029</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARabies&amp;rft.atitle=Failure+to+open+the+blood%93brain+barrier+and+deliver+immune+effectors+to+central+nervous+system+tissues+leads+to+the+lethal+outcome+of+silver-haired+bat+rabies+virus+infection&amp;rft.aufirst=A&amp;rft.au=Hooper%2C+DC&amp;rft.au=Koprowski%2C+H&amp;rft.aulast=Roy&amp;rft.au=Phares%2C+TW&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1797506&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC1797506&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1128%2FJVI.01964-06&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F17108029&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=J.+Virol.&amp;rft.pages=1110-8&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=81"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
